<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Insight into the evolution of the Solanaceae from the parental genomes of Petunia hybrida"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Plants 2016 2:6"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2016-05-27"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2016 Macmillan Publishers Limited"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Petunia hybrida is a popular bedding plant that has a long history as a genetic model system. We report the whole-genome sequencing and assembly of inbred derivatives of its two wild parents, P. axillaris N and P. inflata S6. The assemblies include 91.3% and 90.2% coverage of their diploid genomes (1.4 Gb; 2n&#8201;=&#8201;14) containing 32,928 and 36,697 protein-coding genes, respectively. The genomes reveal that the Petunia lineage has experienced at least two rounds of hexaploidization: the older gamma event, which is shared with most Eudicots, and a more recent Solanaceae event that is shared with tomato and other solanaceous species. Transcription factors involved in the shift from bee to moth pollination reside in particularly dynamic regions of the genome, which may have been key to the remarkable diversity of floral colour patterns and pollination systems. The high-quality genome sequences will enhance the value of Petunia as a model system for research on unique biological phenomena such as small RNAs, symbiosis, self-incompatibility and circadian rhythms. Two high-quality genomes of petunia wild parents reveal two rounds of hexaploidization in the evolution of Petunia lineage and provide insights into the diversity of floral patterns and pollination systems &#8212; enhancing the model value of this genus."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Petunia hybrida</i> is a popular bedding plant that has a long history as a genetic model system. We report the whole-genome sequencing and assembly of inbred derivatives of its two wild parents, <i>P. axillaris</i> N and <i>P. inflata</i> S6. The assemblies include 91.3% and 90.2% coverage of their diploid genomes (1.4 Gb; 2<i>n</i> = 14) containing 32,928 and 36,697 protein-coding genes, respectively. The genomes reveal that the <i>Petunia</i> lineage has experienced at least two rounds of hexaploidization: the older gamma event, which is shared with most Eudicots, and a more recent Solanaceae event that is shared with tomato and other solanaceous species. Transcription factors involved in the shift from bee to moth pollination reside in particularly dynamic regions of the genome, which may have been key to the remarkable diversity of floral colour patterns and pollination systems. The high-quality genome sequences will enhance the value of <i>Petunia</i> as a model system for research on unique biological phenomena such as small RNAs, symbiosis, self-incompatibility and circadian rhythms.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <div class="c-article-section__content"><p>The garden petunia, <i>Petunia hybrida</i>, with its diversity of colour and morphology is the world's most popular bedding plant with an annual wholesale value exceeding US$130 million in the USA alone<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="National Agricultural Statistics Service. Floriculture Crops 2014 Summary (US Department of Agriculture, 2015); 
                    http://usda.mannlib.cornell.edu/usda/nass/FlorCrop//2010s/2015/FlorCrop-06-04-2015.pdf
                    
                  " href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d19403e1505">1</a></sup>. <i>Petunia</i> has a long history as a model species for scientific research. To the scientific community, <i>Petunia</i> is best known for the discovery of RNAi<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Napoli, C., Lemieux, C. &amp; Jorgensen, R. Introduction of chimeric chalcone synthase gene into Petunia results in reversible co-suppression of homologous genes in trans. Plant Cell 2, 279–289 (1990)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d19403e1515">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="van der Krol, A. R., Mur, L. A., Beld, M., Mol, J. N. M. &amp; Stuitje, A. R. Flavonoid genes in Petunia: addition of a limited number of gene copies may lead to a suppression of gene expression. Plant Cell 2, 291–299 (1990)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d19403e1518">3</a></sup>. This breakthrough was the culmination of decades-long research on the synthesis and regulation of the floral pigments and as a consequence anthocyanin biosynthesis remains one of the best-known pathways of secondary metabolism in any plant species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Sheehan, H., Hermann, K. &amp; Kuhlemeier, C. Color and scent: how single genes influence pollinator attraction. Cold Spring Harb. Symp. Quant. Biol. 77, 117–133 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d19403e1523">4</a></sup>. Development, transposon activity, genetic self-incompatibility, and interactions with microbes, herbivores and pollinators have also been active research topics utilizing <i>Petunia</i> as model system.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>The genus <i>Petunia</i> is a member of the Solanaceae family native to South America. It forms a separate and early branching clade within the family with a base chromosome number of <i>x</i> = 7 rather than the typical <i>x</i> = 12 found for most Solanaceae crown-group species, including important crops such as tomato, potato, tobacco, pepper and eggplant<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Saerkinen, T., Bohs, L., Olmstead, R. G. &amp; Knapp, S. A phylogenetic framework for evolutionary study of the nightshades (Solanaceae) a dated 1000-tip tree. BMC Evol Biol 13, 214 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d19403e1542">5</a></sup>. The commercial <i>P. hybrida</i> is derived from crosses between a white-flowered, moth-pollinated <i>P. axillaris</i>, and species of the <i>P. integrifolia</i> clade, a group of closely related bee-pollinated species and subspecies (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Segatto, A. L. A., Ramos-Fregonezi, A. M. C., Bonatto, S. L. &amp; Freitas, L. B. Molecular insights into the purple-flowered ancestor of garden petunias. Am. J. Bot. 101, 119–127 (2014)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d19403e1559">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Stehmann, J. R., Lorenz-Lemke, A. P., Freitas, L. B. &amp; Semir, J. in Petunia: Evolutionary, Developmental and Physiological Genetics 2nd edn (eds Gerats, T. &amp; Strommer, J. ) 1–28 (Springer, 2009)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d19403e1562">7</a></sup>. The first hybrids were produced by European horticulturalists in the early nineteenth century, probably multiple times from different accessions of the two parent clades<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Stehmann, J. R., Lorenz-Lemke, A. P., Freitas, L. B. &amp; Semir, J. in Petunia: Evolutionary, Developmental and Physiological Genetics 2nd edn (eds Gerats, T. &amp; Strommer, J. ) 1–28 (Springer, 2009)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d19403e1566">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Sink, K. C. in Petunia: Monographs on Theoretical and Applied Genetics Vol. 9 (ed. Sink, K. C. ) 3–9 (Springer, 1984)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d19403e1569">8</a></sup>. The remarkable phenotypic diversity in today's commercial garden petunias is the result of almost two centuries of intense commercial breeding. Here, we present the genome sequences of <i>P. axillaris</i> N and <i>P. inflata</i> S6, two inbred laboratory accessions representing the parents of <i>P. hybrida</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Origin and diversity of P. hybrida flowers."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Origin and diversity of <i>P. hybrida</i> flowers.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201674/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.74/MediaObjects/41477_2016_Article_BFnplants201674_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.74/MediaObjects/41477_2016_Article_BFnplants201674_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1214"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, <i>P. inflata</i> S6, <i>P. axillaris</i> N and their F1. <b>b</b>, Selected individuals from <i>P. inflata</i> S6 × <i>P. axillaris</i> N F2 population. <b>c</b>, Commercial <i>P. hybrida</i> accessions. <b>d</b>, <i>P. hybrida</i> accessions and mutants. Row 1, from left to right: Mitchell (W115); R27; transposon line W138; R143; vacuolar <i>ph3</i> mutant with pale colour compared with the isogenic R143. Mitchell, R27 and R143 were used for transcriptomics analysis. Row 2, from left to right: V26; V26 with CHS RNAi transgene (images provided by J. Kooter, VU Amsterdam); homeotic mutant <i>pMADS3RNAi/fbp6</i>; <i>an2</i> mutant; homeotic mutant <i>blind</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201674/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div><section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Results and discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Results and discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec2">Sequencing, assembly and annotation</h3><p>For <i>P. axillaris</i> N, we performed a hybrid <i>de novo</i> assembly using a combination of short read (Illumina; coverage 137X) and long read technologies (PacBio; coverage 21X), whereas for <i>P. inflata</i> S6 we produced exclusively short reads (Illumina; coverage 135X) and performed a short read <i>de novo</i> assembly (for details see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#MOESM9">Supplementary Note 1</a>). The resulting high-quality assemblies have a size of 1.26 Gb for <i>P. axillaris</i> and 1.29 Gb for <i>P. inflata</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Tab1">Table 1</a>). The estimated size of both genomes is 1.4 Gb, using a k-mer size of 31, which is consistent with previous microdensity measurements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="White, J. &amp; Rees, H. Chromosome weights and measures in Petunia. Heredity 58, 139–143 (1987)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d19403e1689">9</a></sup>. We have remapped Illumina reads to the assemblies and called single nucleotide polymorphism (SNPs) to estimate the level of heterozygosity, which is estimated as 0.03% for both accessions. Moreover, we mapped the 248 Core Eukaryotic Genes (CEGs) to assess the completeness of both assemblies and found 239 (94%) and 243 (98%) in the assembly of <i>P. axillaris</i> and <i>P. inflata</i>, respectively. The estimated unassembled fraction of the genome comprises <span class="stix">∼</span>140 Mb for <i>P. axillaris</i> (181 Mb if sequence gaps of 41 Mb are included) and <span class="stix">∼</span>110 Mb for <i>P. inflata</i> (197 Mb with sequence gaps of 87 Mb), which is likely to be due to the large numbers of repetitive sequences (see below). Genome annotation identified 32,928 protein-coding genes for <i>P. axillaris</i> and 36,697 protein-coding genes for <i>P. inflata</i> with an average of 5.2 and 5.1 exons per protein coding gene and an average predicted protein size of 393 and 386 amino acids, respectively.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary statistics of the genome assemblies.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nplants201674/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Repeat landscape of <i>Petunia</i> genomes</h3><p><i>Petunia</i> genomes are rich in repetitive DNA (as are most other plant genomes), but its presence at 60–65% of the assembled genome is relatively low considering its genome size (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#MOESM9">Supplementary Note 2</a>), indicating a larger gene, regulatory and low copy sequence space. Long terminal repeats (LTR)-retroelement-related sequences are abundant near centromeres (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>), and within the assemblies, equal numbers of fragments and full-length Ty3/Gypsy-like and Ty1/Copia-like elements were detected. Repeat cluster analysis of unassembled reads supported the amount and complexity of the diverse and rearranged repeat landscape of <i>Petunia</i>. <i>Petunia</i> chromosomes average 200 Mb in length (three times that of <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> or <i>S. tuberosum</i>), as a larger genome is distributed over 7 rather than 12 chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). Chromosomal organization in <i>Petunia</i> is thus different compared to other Solanaceae and this together with high DNA transposon frequency and mobility has an effect on genome evolution, meiotic recombination and homogenization events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Heslop-Harrison, J. P. Genome evolution: extinction, continuation or explosion? Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 15, 115–121 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d19403e1993">10</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Genome and repeat organization."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Genome and repeat organization.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201674/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.74/MediaObjects/41477_2016_Article_BFnplants201674_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.74/MediaObjects/41477_2016_Article_BFnplants201674_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="859"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Comparative genome organization of <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>, <i>P. axillaris</i> and <i>Nicotiana tomentosiformis</i>. The circles are proportional to genome size; regulatory sequences and repeat classes are shown in the segments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2020">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Sierro, N. et al. Reference genomes and transcriptomes of Nicotiana sylvestris and Nicotiana tomentosiformis. Genome Biol. 14, R60 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2023">29</a></sup>. <b>b</b>, Fluorescent <i>in situ</i> hybridization (FISH) to <i>P. axillaris</i> chromosomes (grey). Red: four pericentromeric <i>Petunia vein clearing virus</i> (PVCV) sites; green: dispersed <i>Gypsy</i>-like retroelement junction probe at all centromeres (overlapping yellow signals); blue: 5S rDNA. Scale bar, 10 µm. <b>c</b>, Distribution of <i>dTph1-</i>like transposons in <i>P. axillaris</i> and <i>P. inflata</i>. <b>d</b>, Duplicated gene families in functional categories showing <i>Petunia</i>-specific and balanced families. <b>e</b>, Venn diagram based on the gene family cluster analysis from five Solanaceae species. The numbers below the species name indicate the number of protein-coding genes (top) and number of gene family clusters (bottom).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201674/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">DNA transposons</h3><p>DNA transposons are five times more abundant in the <i>Petunia</i> genome than in <i>Nicotiana tomentosiformis</i> and <i>S. lycopersicum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>). The identification and cloning of the small endogenous non-autonomous <i>h</i>AT-like <i>defective transposon of petunia hybrida1</i> (<i>dTph1</i>), which is highly mobile in the <i>P. hybrida</i> line W138 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>d), has allowed the development of efficient tools for forward and reverse genetics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Vandenbussche, M. et al. Generation of a 3D indexed Petunia insertion database for reverse genetics. Plant J. 54, 1105–1114 (2008)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2109">11</a></sup>. The <i>P. axillaris</i> and <i>P. inflata</i> genomes contain 16 and 21 <i>dTph1</i> copies, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#MOESM9">Supplementary Note 3</a>). This is similar to the numbers in most <i>P. hybrida</i> accessions, but far fewer than in the old <i>P. hybrida</i> accession R27 or the hyperactive accession W138 with over 200 copies. Comparison of <i>dTph1</i> insertion loci in <i>P. axillaris</i> and <i>P. inflata</i> with W138 provides evidence that both species indeed contributed to W138. <i>dTph1</i> distribution patterns in wild <i>P. axillaris</i> accessions from Uruguay showed comparable low <i>dTph1</i> copy numbers and a very low overall locus diversity, suggesting that <i>dTph1</i> transposition activity is largely suppressed in natural populations, but was reactivated after the interspecific crosses leading to the domesticated <i>P. hybrida</i>. Seven previously identified <i>dTph1</i>-like elements and one newly discovered element, <i>dTPh12</i>, are present in both genomes, demonstrating their ancient origin (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>.) The expansion of different transposable elements—<i>dTph1</i> in W138 and <i>dTph7</i> in the two wild species—suggests that, despite extensive homology in their terminal inverted repeat regions, they may require different transacting factors for their mobility.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Endogenous pararetroviruses</h3><p>Integrated copies of <i>Caulimoviridae</i> are widespread in plant nuclear genomes including the Solanaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Geering, A. D. W. et al. Endogenous florendoviruses are major components of plant genomes and hallmarks of virus evolution. Nature Commun. 5, 5269 (2014)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2188">12</a></sup>. These DNA viruses are characterized by a <i>gag</i> region with RNA binding domains and a <i>pol</i> region that codes for reverse transcriptase and RNase H (ref. 13). The <i>P. axillaris</i> and <i>P. inflata</i> genomes show near-complete but also degenerated and rearranged copies of <i>Petunia vein clearing virus</i> (PVCV, a <i>Petuvirus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Richert-Pöggeler, K. R., Noreen, F., Schwarzacher, T., Harper, G. &amp; Hohn, T. Induction of infectious petunia vein clearing (pararetro) virus from endogenous provirus in petunia. EMBO J. 22, 4836–4845 (2003)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2210">14</a></sup>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#MOESM9">Supplementary Note 2</a>). Their structures suggest that the behaviour and mode of integration are similar for both species, and parallel the types of complex rearrangements seen in the banana genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="D'Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2217">15</a></sup>. Fluorescent <i>in situ</i> hybridization of these sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>) showed signals near the centromeres of two chromosome pairs in <i>P. axillaris</i> adjacent to LTR retroelements. Phylogenetic analysis of single insertions showed repeated incidents of homogenization. Such homologous sequences contributed to the tandem array structures found in <i>P. hybrida</i> that are prerequisites of inducible and disease generating viruses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Richert-Pöggeler, K. R., Noreen, F., Schwarzacher, T., Harper, G. &amp; Hohn, T. Induction of infectious petunia vein clearing (pararetro) virus from endogenous provirus in petunia. EMBO J. 22, 4836–4845 (2003)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2234">14</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Gene families and tandem duplications</h3><p>Polypeptide sequences from <i>P. axillaris</i>, <i>P. inflata</i>, <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>S. tuberosum</i>, <i>Nicotiana benthamiana</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> were clustered into gene families. This analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#MOESM9">Supplementary Note 4</a>) grouped 39.2% of the genes into 27,600 gene families, ranging in size from 2 to 1,026 members. Most gene families followed the accepted evolutionary lineage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>), with the <i>Petunia</i>, <i>Solanum</i> and Solanaceae clades sharing gene families far more often than other species groupings (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig2">Fig. 2e</a>). Two contrasting sets of gene families that are almost mutually exclusive were found: <i>Petunia</i>-specific families and balanced shared families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig2">Fig. 2d</a>). The size distributions of tandem gene arrays in <i>P. axillaris</i>, <i>P. inflata</i> and <i>S. lycopersicum</i> were quite similar, with each species containing about 8,000 genes in 3,000 tandem arrays.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Genome triplication and fractionation in Petunia."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Genome triplication and fractionation in <i>Petunia.</i></b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201674/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.74/MediaObjects/41477_2016_Article_BFnplants201674_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.74/MediaObjects/41477_2016_Article_BFnplants201674_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="894"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Paleohexapolyploid history of the Solanaceae family, showing the gamma hexaploidy event shared with most eudicots and the family-specific Solanaceae-α hexaploidy event. We place Solanaceae-α before the divergence of <i>Petunia</i> and the <i>x</i> = 12 crown-group (<span class="stix">∼</span>30 and 49 Myr ago (Ma))<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Saerkinen, T., Bohs, L., Olmstead, R. G. &amp; Knapp, S. A phylogenetic framework for evolutionary study of the nightshades (Solanaceae) a dated 1000-tip tree. BMC Evol Biol 13, 214 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2320">5</a></sup>. <b>b</b>, Differential gene fractionation of <i>Petunia</i> (<i>P. axillaris</i>, shortened to <i>P. axi</i>.) and tomato (<i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>S. lyco.</i>) in comparison with grape (<i>V. vitifera</i>). One grape genomic region is syntenic to three regions of <i>Petunia</i> and tomato. Genes in red represent shared-retained genes of <i>Petunia</i> and tomato whereas green (retained in <i>Petunia</i>/lost in tomato) and purple (retained in tomato/lost in <i>Petunia</i>) represent independently fractionated genes. For details see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#MOESM9">Supplementary Note 5</a>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201674/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Paleopolyploidy history of <i>Petunia</i></h3><p>Analysis of the <i>Petunia</i> data allowed us to infer the history of polyploidy not only for <i>Petunia</i> but for the entire Solanaceae. Polyploidy is ubiquitous among angiosperms, with many independent lineage-specific paleopolyploidy events associated with changes in genome structure and gene retention and loss<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Ancestral polyploidy in seed plants and angiosperms. Nature 473, 97–100 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2387">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Soltis, D. E., Bell, C. D., Kim, S. &amp; Soltis, P. S. Origin and early evolution of angiosperms. Ann. NY Acad. Sci. 1133, 3–25 (2008)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2390">17</a></sup>. Most paleopolyploidy events are the result of ancient genome duplications (paleotetraploidies), but ancient triplications (paleohexaploidies) have also been identified, for example the gamma event near the origin of Eudicots (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>) first detected by analysis of the <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (grape) genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2401">18</a></sup>. Similarly, genome analysis of <i>S. lycopersicum</i> suggested that there was a triplication at some point during the evolution of the Solanaceae family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2408">19</a></sup>. <i>Petunia</i> as a sister to the <i>x</i> = 12 crown-group clade of the Solanaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Saerkinen, T., Bohs, L., Olmstead, R. G. &amp; Knapp, S. A phylogenetic framework for evolutionary study of the nightshades (Solanaceae) a dated 1000-tip tree. BMC Evol Biol 13, 214 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2418">5</a></sup> is an ideal species to investigate the timing and nature of this event (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>).</p><p>Using whole-genome synteny analyses of our <i>de novo</i> assemblies, we identified genomic regions of collinearity between <i>S. lycopersicum</i> and <i>P. axillaris</i>, using <i>V. vinifera</i> as an outgroup (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#MOESM9">Supplementary Note 5</a>). Inferring their relative timing by analysing synonymous changes (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>), we show that <i>Petunia</i> shares the older gamma paleopolyploidy event with other higher eudicots, and the more recent paleohexaploidy event with <i>S. lycopersicum</i>. We then can infer that the Solanaceae event occurred at least 30 Myr ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). Microsynteny analysis shows the process of gene fractionation following the polyploidization event, and reveals that the <i>S. lycopersicum</i> genome has retained fewer genes than the <i>Petunia</i> genome, thus contributing to the relatively large genic fraction found in <i>Petunia</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>). From the fractionation patterns observed, (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>), we predict a first and common incomplete gene fractionation step in both <i>Petunia</i> and <i>S. lycopersicum</i> and a second step after their divergence in <i>S. lycopersicum</i> only. This may have contributed to the separation of the lineages, similar to that observed in <i>Saccharomyces</i> yeasts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Scannell, D. R., Byrne, K. P., Gordon, J. L., Wong, S. &amp; Wolfe, K. H. Multiple rounds of speciation associated with reciprocal gene loss in polyploid yeasts. Nature 440, 341–345 (2006)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2487">20</a></sup> but until now not yet described in flowering plants.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Origin of the <i>P. hybrida</i> genomes</h3><p>Comparisons of the two genome sequences with transcriptomics data from three unrelated <i>P. hybrida</i> lines, namely Mitchell, R27 and R143 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>d, see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#MOESM9">Supplementary Note 6</a>) revealed a complex history of the garden petunia. The majority of the <span class="stix">∼</span>20,000 analysed genes could be assigned to <i>P. axillaris</i> (<span class="stix">∼</span>15,000), with only <span class="stix">∼</span>600 genes assigned to <i>P. inflata</i>. This indicates that the <i>P. inflata</i> parent makes only a minor contribution to the <i>P. hybrida</i> gene space. One possible explanation for this preponderance of the white parent genome could be that breeding for different colours and colour patterns required a background with recessive mutations in the pigmentation pathway. About 2,000 <i>P. hybrida</i> genes contain a high percentage of non-specific SNPs potentially derived from an unknown ancestor.</p><p>Approximately 1,500 genes of mixed parentage were identified, with blocks of SNPs similar to <i>P. axillaris</i> and other blocks similar to <i>P. inflata</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). These unusual constellations are conserved between the three <i>P. hybrida</i> accessions and may involve gene conversion, random repair of heteroduplexes, contributions of unknown parents or unknown mechanisms. Gene conversion events have been previously reported in plastids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Khakhlova, O. &amp; Bock, R. Elimination of deleterious mutations in plastid genomes by gene conversion. Plant J. 46, 85–94 (2006)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2543">21</a></sup> and polyploids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Salmon, A., Udall, J. A., Jeddeloh, J. A. &amp; Wendel, J. Targeted capture of homoeologous coding and noncoding sequence in polyploid cotton. G3 2, 921–930 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2548">22</a></sup> but they have not been reported before in hybrids (or species of hybrid origin). Definitive answers, especially to the question whether this phenomenon is restricted to transcribed regions will require transcriptome and whole-genome sequencing of multiple <i>P. hybrida</i> accessions.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="A large fraction of P. hybrida genes may be the result of gene conversion."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: A large fraction of <i>P. hybrida</i> genes may be the result of gene conversion.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201674/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.74/MediaObjects/41477_2016_Article_BFnplants201674_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.74/MediaObjects/41477_2016_Article_BFnplants201674_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="474"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>,<b>b</b>, Two examples of genes with mixed parentage in <i>P. hybrida</i> accessions Mitchell, R143 and R27. <b>a</b>, PME inhibitor; <i>Peaxi162Scf00002g00042</i> and <i>Peinf101Scf01857g01001</i> for <i>P. axillaris</i> N and <i>P. inflata</i> S6, respectively. <b>b</b>, Stress-induced phosphoprotein; <i>Peaxi162Scf00002g00511</i> and <i>Peinf101Scf01857g08047</i> for <i>P. axillaris</i> N and <i>P. inflata</i> S6, respectively. Green and blue circles represent SNPs specific to <i>P. axillaris</i> N and <i>P. inflata</i> S6, respectively. Small black arrows represent SNPs present only in the <i>P. hybrida</i> lines.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201674/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Genes encoding pollinator attraction traits</h3><p>Bee-pollinated <i>P. inflata</i> has purple flowers that produce only a limited amount of scent, whereas the flowers of the hawkmoth-pollinated <i>P. axillaris</i> are strongly scented and white (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>a). Colour and scent influence the attraction of pollinators and thereby cause reproductive isolation and ultimately speciation. Speciation of <i>P. axillaris</i> from a <i>P. inflata-</i>like ancestor involved the loss of anthocyanin pigments and the gain of volatiles<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Sheehan, H., Hermann, K. &amp; Kuhlemeier, C. Color and scent: how single genes influence pollinator attraction. Cold Spring Harb. Symp. Quant. Biol. 77, 117–133 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2652">4</a></sup>. Thus the genes that caused the changes in these two traits are potential speciation genes. The anthocyanin backbone is synthesized from phenylalanine by nine enzymatic steps followed by specific decorations of the backbone that modify the absorption spectrum. To address how the change in anthocyanin pigmentation of <i>Petunia</i> flowers evolved, we compared all known regulatory and structural genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#MOESM9">Supplementary Note 7</a>).</p><p>Both <i>Petunia</i> genomes contain a complete set of functional genes for the core pathway (<i>CHS</i>, <i>CHI</i>, <i>DFR</i>, <i>ANS</i>, <i>3GT</i>, <i>5GT</i> and <i>AAT)</i>; however, some of the decorating enzymes are compromised in <i>P. axillaris</i>. The steps in the pathway, from DFR on, are regulated by a ternary complex consisting of MYB, bHLH and WD40 transcription factors. The bHLH and WD40 components are functional, but in all <i>P. axillaris</i> accessions, the MYB factor AN2 has been inactivated because of independent mutations in the coding region<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Quattrocchio, F. et al. Molecular analysis of the anthocyanin2 gene of Petunia and its role in the evolution of flower color. Plant Cell 11, 1433–1444 (1999)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2696">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Hoballah, M. E. et al. Single gene-mediated shift in pollinator attraction in Petunia. Plant Cell 19, 779–790 (2007)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2699">24</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>d). The only known function of AN2 is to regulate anthocyanin synthesis in petal lobes and this lack of pleiotropic effects makes <i>AN2</i> a preferred target of selection in the natural habitat.</p><p>In <i>P. hybrida</i>, four related MYB factors activate the anthocyanin biosynthetic pathway in different tissues: AN2 controls anthocyanin deposition in the petal limb, AN4 in the anthers and DPL and PHZ in green tissues. Unlike <i>AN2</i>, the <i>AN4</i>, <i>DPL</i> and <i>PHZ</i> coding sequences have remained intact in <i>P. axillaris</i>. Based on <i>P. hybrida</i> data, differential expression of <i>AN4</i> might be responsible for the shift in anther colour from purple in <i>P. inflata</i> to yellow in <i>P. axillaris.</i></p><p>The genomic regions containing these four <i>MYB</i> genes have undergone massive rearrangements since the separation of the two species estimated at 0.9 Myr ago, possibly influenced by transposon or retroelement activities found in the vicinity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a>). As a consequence, the synteny between the corresponding regions of <i>P. axillaris</i> and <i>P. inflata</i> has been largely destroyed and gene spacing altered. <i>P. axillaris AN4</i> is duplicated and inactivated subsequently in anthers because of large insertions of transposon-like sequences in the promoter. Similar insertion events are visible around the other anthocyanin <i>MYB</i> genes. Instead, the genomic regions containing other anthocyanin regulators (<i>AN1</i>, <i>JAF13</i>, <i>AN11</i>) and other MYBs involved in vacuolar pH regulation and scent production show strong conservation of the synteny between the two <i>Petunia</i> species. Thus, the <i>AN2</i>-like MYBs reside in an exceptionally dynamic region of the genome. Although lack of pleiotropy makes <i>AN2</i>-like MYBs preferential targets of selection, genomic rearrangements may have provided the mechanism responsible for the remarkable spatial and temporal diversity of anthocyanin pigmentation patterns.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Pollinator attraction."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Pollinator attraction.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201674/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.74/MediaObjects/41477_2016_Article_BFnplants201674_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.74/MediaObjects/41477_2016_Article_BFnplants201674_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="400"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Genome dynamics at different <i>MYB</i> gene regions. Genomic regions around <i>AN2</i>-like genes are highly rearranged with few conserved genes, whereas synteny is conserved around the MYB <i>ODO1</i> involved in scent production. Black arrows, <i>MYB</i> genes. Different coloured arrows, other syntenic genes. Purple blocks, various repeat sequences. <b>b</b>, Biosynthesis of 2-phenylacetaldehyde is different in <i>Petunia</i> and <i>S. lycopersicum</i>. Red and blue arrows depict enzymatic steps characterized in <i>S. lycopersicum</i> and <i>Petunia</i>, respectively. The black arrow represents a predicted activity in <i>S. lycopersicum</i>. <b>c</b>, Biosynthesis of eugenol in <i>Petunia</i>. Although tomato also makes eugenol, homologues of the two genes involved seem to be absent. AADC, aromatic <span class="u-small-caps">l</span>-amino acid decarboxylase; PAAS, phenylacetaldehyde synthase; CFAT, CoA:coniferyl alcohol acetyltransferase; EGS, eugenol synthase.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201674/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Exceptional dynamics of the regions containing the MYB regulators of the anthocyanin pathway is not restricted to <i>Petunia</i>. The regions in <i>S. lycopersicum</i> share little synteny with either of the two <i>Petunia</i> species indicating that large rearrangements occurred after the separation of the genera. In the more distantly related <i>Mimulus guttatus</i>, we also find duplications and rearrangements to have taken place after the separation of the ancestors of Solanaceae and Phrymaceae. Thus, genome dynamics of <i>AN2-</i>type MYB factors may be a general mechanism that caused the diversity of floral pigmentation patterns across angiosperms.</p><p><i>P. axillaris</i> emits an abundant blend of floral benzenoid and phenylpropanoid volatiles whereas <i>P. inflata</i> only emits benzaldehyde. A comparison of all structural and regulatory genes known to be involved in floral scent synthesis indicates that all the known biosynthetic and regulatory genes encode functional proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#MOESM9">Supplementary Note 8</a>). Thus, the increase in complexity and concentration of volatiles accompanying the shift to moth pollination in <i>P. axillaris</i> involved mutations in <i>cis</i>-acting regulatory elements or the mutation of as yet unknown transcriptional regulators.</p><p><i>Petunia</i> uses a single enzyme for the biosynthesis of 2-phenylactealdehyde<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Kaminaga, Y. et al. Plant phenylacetaldehyde synthase is a bifunctional homotetrameric enzyme that catalyzes phenylalanine decarboxylation and oxidation. J. Biol. Chem. 281, 23357–23366 (2006)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2888">25</a></sup> whereas <i>S. lycopersicum</i> utilizes an amino acid decarboxylase plus a yet unidentified amine oxidase (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig5">Fig. 5b</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Tieman, D. et al. Tomato aromatic amino acid decarboxylases participate in synthesis of the flavor volatiles 2-phenylethanol and 2-phenylacetaldehyde. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 103, 8287–8292 (2006)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2898">26</a></sup>. Interestingly, the <i>S. lycopersicum</i> genome does harbour a homologue of the <i>Petunia</i> gene, but this is predicted to be 124 amino acids shorter than its <i>Petunia</i> homologue and presumably inactive. Furthermore, although <i>S. lycopersicum</i> is also known to produce eugenol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Tikunov, Y. M. et al. Non-smoky glycosyltransferase1 prevents the release of smoky aroma from tomato fruit. Plant Cell 25, 3067–3078 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2915">27</a></sup>, homologues of the two involved enzymes appear to be absent (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig5">Fig. 5c</a>). Thus, the Solanaceae have evolved multiple strategies for the synthesis of C6–C2 and C6–C3 compounds.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10"><i>Petunia</i> as a model for comparative research of gene function</h3><p>High throughput DNA sequencing makes it possible to compare DNA sequences and RNA expression patterns across a wide variety of taxa. However, functional analysis is necessary to determine if sequence conservation can be equated with conservation of gene function. Good examples are the <i>AP2</i> and <i>BL/FIS</i> (<i>MIR169</i>) genes, which, although very well conserved at the sequence level, can perform divergent developmental functions in different species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Cartolano, M. et al. A conserved microRNA module exerts homeotic control over Petunia hybrida and Antirrhinum majus floral organ identity. Nature Genet. 39, 901–905 (2007)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d19403e2942">28</a></sup>.</p><p>In general, a larger diversity of genetic model systems will be essential to link sequence information with function. Ease of cultivation and propagation, highly efficient genetics and transformation make <i>Petunia</i> an attractive model system for comparative analysis of gene function (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Sec11">Boxes&nbsp;1</a><a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Sec12">boxed-text</a><a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Sec13">boxed-text</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Sec14">4</a>). The availability of high-quality genome sequences further increases the utility of the asterid <i>Petunia</i> not only for testing the generality of conclusions based on the rosid <i>Arabidopsis</i> or the monocot rice, but also for studying biological phenomena in a species with different genome organization, biochemistry, development, ecology and evolution.</p><aside><div class="c-article-box" data-expandable-box-container="true"><div class="c-article-box__container" data-expandable-box="true" aria-hidden="true" id="box-Sec11"><h3 class="c-article-box__container-title u-h3 js-expandable-title" id="Sec11">Box 1: MicroRNAs.</h3><div class="c-article-box__content"><p>Small RNA sequencing of young flower buds and mapping of candidates in the genome sequences confirm the presence of 44 conserved miRNAs in <i>Petunia</i>, belonging to 30 families and corresponding to 140 <i>MIR</i> loci, in line with other species. Only two loci were unique to <i>P. axillaris</i> (<i>MIR</i>171g, <i>MIR</i>398b) and two unique and one truncated to <i>P. inflata</i> (<i>MIR</i>160d, <i>MIR</i>397b, <i>MIR</i>477), and <i>MIR</i> sequences and organization in the genome were largely conserved. MiRNA expression profiles were also highly similar between the two species and comparable to tomato and potato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3007">19</a></sup>, with highest expressions for miR166, miR159 and miR319, known for their involvement in floral organ development, and low expression for miRNA169c (<i>BLIND</i>), a well-studied regulator of floral whorl identity in <i>Petunia</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>d) and <i>Antirrhinum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Cartolano, M. et al. A conserved microRNA module exerts homeotic control over Petunia hybrida and Antirrhinum majus floral organ identity. Nature Genet. 39, 901–905 (2007)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3023">28</a></sup>. Predicted miRNA targets included genes involved in development and metabolic pathways. For additional information, see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#MOESM9">Supplementary Note 9</a>.</p></div></div></div></aside><aside><div class="c-article-box" data-expandable-box-container="true"><div class="c-article-box__container" data-expandable-box="true" aria-hidden="true" id="box-Sec12"><h3 class="c-article-box__container-title u-h3 js-expandable-title" id="Sec12">Box 2: Symbiosis with fungi.</h3><div class="c-article-box__content"><p>A good example of <i>Petunia</i> as a model system is the study of the symbiotic interactions with fungi. In the arbuscular mycorrhiza (AM) with the Glomeromycota, the fungal hyphae function as an extension of the root system that enhances the acquisition of mineral nutrients, primarily phosphate. This symbiosis is widespread amongst land plants, but is lacking in the Brassicaceae (including <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>). Symbiosis signalling involves a family of lysine motive (LysM) receptor-like kinases (LysM-RLK) in the host, which perceive specific microbial signals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Antolin-Llovera, M. et al. Knowing your friends and foes – plant receptor-like kinases as initiators of symbiosis or defence. New Phytol. 204, 791–802 (2014)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3044">44</a></sup>. LysM-RLKs activate a signalling pathway that is shared with root nodule symbiosis, a symbiotic interaction restricted to the Fabaceae. The comparison between <i>Petunia</i> and tomato versus the legumes <i>Lotus japonicus</i> and <i>Medicago truncatula</i> revealed that the two Solanaceae have considerably smaller gene families than the two legumes with 10/11 and 14 versus 17 and 18 LysM-RLK members, respectively. The expansion of the LysM-RLK family during the evolution of nodule symbiosis in the legumes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Limpens, E., van Zeijl, A. &amp; Geurts, R. Lipochitooligosaccharides modulate plant host immunity to enable endosymbioses. Ann. Rev. Phytopatol. 53, 15.1–15.24 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3058">45</a></sup> will help to understand the evolution of two fundamentally different symbiotic interactions. Large-scale genomic and transcriptional analysis of two transcription factor families revealed that several GRAS genes are regulated during AM symbiosis, whereas <i>AP2</i>/<i>ERF</i> genes are induced during adventitious root formation. For additional information, see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#MOESM9">Supplementary Note 10</a>.</p></div></div></div></aside><aside><div class="c-article-box" data-expandable-box-container="true"><div class="c-article-box__container" data-expandable-box="true" aria-hidden="true" id="box-Sec13"><h3 class="c-article-box__container-title u-h3 js-expandable-title" id="Sec13">Box 3: Self-incompatibility.</h3><div class="c-article-box__content"><p>In the Solanaceae, self-fertilization is prevented by S-RNase-mediated gametophytic self-incompatibility (GSI), which is based on the ability to reject pollen from a plant expressing a matching S-locus haplotype, while accepting pollen from individuals whose haplotypes do not match that of the stylar parent<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Sims, T. L. &amp; Robbins, T. P in Petunia: Evolutionary, Developmental and Physiological Genetics (eds Gerats, T. &amp; Strommer, J. ) 85–106 (Springer, 2009)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3080">46</a></sup>. During an incompatible pollination, the growth of ‘self’ pollen tubes is inhibited by the action of an imported, cognate, ribonuclease, the S-RNase. In compatible pollinations, the action of non-self S-RNases is inhibited by ubiquitination and degradation by a SCF<sup>SLF</sup> E3 ubiquitin ligase, of which distinct SLF (S-locus F-box) proteins are the recognition component.</p><p>We found that the S-RNase in <i>P. axillaris</i> is identical to the S<sub>1</sub>-RNase from <i>P. hybrida</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Clark, K. R., Okuley, J. J., Collins, P. D. &amp; Sims, T. L. Sequence variability and developmental expression of S-alleles in self-incompatible and pseudo-self-compatible Petunia. Plant Cell 2, 815–826 (1990)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3096">47</a></sup>. This finding is consistent with the hypothesis that self-incompatibility in <i>P. hybrida</i> was inherited from both of the progenitor species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Van den Broeck, D. et al. Transposon display identifies individual transposable elements in high copy number lines. Plant J. 13, 121–129 (1998)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3103">43</a></sup>. In addition, we identified 20 SLF genes in the genome of <i>P. axillaris</i> and 29 SLF genes in <i>P. inflata</i>, together representing 20 different SLF variants. Several (2–5) SLF genes in each species were found linked on the same scaffold; in <i>P. axillaris</i>, the S<sub>ax1</sub>-RNase was linked to SLF10. These data support current models, which propose that the pollen and style components of GSI are tightly linked in a region of the chromosome that is suppressed for recombination. Suppression of recombination is an important mechanism to preserve co-adapted gene complexes. Our long-range sequencing strategy is an important step towards characterizing a complete S-locus, as a means to better understand the evolution of gametophytic self-incompatibility. For additional information, see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#MOESM9">Supplementary Note 11</a>.</p></div></div></div></aside><aside><div class="c-article-box" data-expandable-box-container="true"><div class="c-article-box__container" data-expandable-box="true" aria-hidden="true" id="box-Sec14"><h3 class="c-article-box__container-title u-h3 js-expandable-title" id="Sec14">Box 4: The circadian clock.</h3><div class="c-article-box__content"><p>Floral volatiles serve to attract pollinators but will also be perceived by unwanted herbivores<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Kessler, D., Diezel, C., Clark, D. G., Colquhoun, T. A. &amp; Baldwin, I. T. Petunia flowers solve the defence/apparency dilemma of pollinator attraction by deploying complex floral blends. Ecol. Lett. 16, 299–306 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3130">48</a></sup>. Volatile emission in <i>P. axillaris</i> is under circadian control, peaking at dusk when its nocturnal hawkmoth pollinator visits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Sponberg, S., Dyhr, J. P., Hall, R. W. &amp; Daniel, T. L. Luminance-dependent visual processing enables moth flight in low light. Science 348, 1245–1248 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3137">49</a></sup>. Although this specific output is known quite well, the genetic structure of the circadian clock itself is understudied in <i>Petunia</i>. In <i>Arabidopsis,</i> the clock consists of three loops, the morning, core and evening loop, based on their expression pattern. The current hypothesis is that gene dosage effect is important for clock function<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Lou, P. et al. Genetic architecture of the circadian clock and flowering time in Brassica rapa. Theor. Appl. Genet. 123, 397–409 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3148">50</a></sup>. Comparison of the <i>Petunia</i> genomes with other Solanaceae indicates that the circadian clock has undergone a deep restructuring in the Solanaceae and each species seems to have a different set of genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#MOESM9">Supplementary Note 12</a>). <i>Petunia</i> and the rest of the Solanaceae share single orthologues for <i>LHY</i>, <i>TOC1</i>, <i>PRR3</i> and the MYB transcription factor <i>LUX</i>. In contrast <i>PRR7</i>, <i>PRR5</i>, <i>GIGANTEA</i>, <i>ELF3</i> and <i>ELF4</i> are in some cases duplicated or triplicated. <i>P. inflata</i> has a larger number of evening loop paralogues than <i>P. axillaris</i>. The <i>GI</i> gene is present in two copies in <i>P. axillaris</i> with three in <i>P. inflata</i>, and there are three <i>ELF3</i> copies in <i>P. axillaris</i> and four in <i>P. inflata</i>. These results suggest strong purifying selection on some of the clock components but others may have undergone a rapid subfunctionalization or redeployment. Comparative analyses will help to understand how clock structure is adjusted to optimize specific outputs thus allowing adaptation to different environments. For additional information, see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#MOESM9">Supplementary Note 12</a>.</p></div></div></div></aside></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec15" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec15-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec15">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec15-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Genome sequencing, assembly and annotation</h3><p>Plants were grown and DNA was extracted following the methods described at <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#MOESM9">Supplementary Note 1</a>.</p><p>Illumina libraries with 0.17-, 0.35-, 0.5-, 0.8-, 1-, 2-, 5-, 8- and 15-kb inserts were sequenced at BGI-Shenzghen and University of Illinois, Roy J. Carver Biotechnology. PacBio <i>P. axillaris</i> DNA library was sequenced with P4/C2 chemistry.</p><p>Illumina reads were processed using Fastq-mcf (quality filtering; <a href="https://code.google.com/p/ea-utils/wiki/FastqMcf">https://code.google.com/p/ea-utils/wiki/FastqMcf</a>), PRINSEQ (duplication filtering; <a href="http://prinseq.sourceforge.net/">http://prinseq.sourceforge.net/</a>) and Musket (error correction; <a href="http://musket.sourceforge.net/">http://musket.sourceforge.net/</a>). Pacbio reads were processed using the SMRT Analysis pipeline (v.2.0.1; <a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/SMRT-Analysis">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/SMRT-Analysis</a>).</p><p>Both genomes were assembled with SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3275">30</a></sup> with different k-mer sizes. For both genomes, k-mer = 79 showed the best statistics. Gaps between contigs were completed using GapCloser<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3279">30</a></sup>. Additionally for <i>P. axillaris</i>, PacBio reads were integrated in four different steps: (1) Rescaffolding of the Illumina contigs using the PacBio reads and the AHA assembler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Bashir, A. et al. A hybrid approach for the automated finishing of bacterial genomes. Nature Biotechnol. 30, 701–707 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3286">31</a></sup>; (2) Gap filling using PBJelly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="English, A. C. et al. Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with pacific biosciences RS long-Read sequencing technology. PloS One 7, e47768 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3290">32</a></sup>; (3) Rescaffolding using the Illumina pair data and SSPACE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V., Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578–579 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3295">33</a></sup>; (4) Last round of gap filling using PBJelly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="English, A. C. et al. Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with pacific biosciences RS long-Read sequencing technology. PloS One 7, e47768 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3299">32</a></sup>.</p><p>Genome size estimation was performed through the k-mers abundance distribution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Li, R. et al. The sequence and de novo assembly of the giant panda genome. Nature 463, 311–317 (2010)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3307">34</a></sup> (k-mer = 31). Heterozygosity was estimated mapping the Illumina reads to the assemblies using Bowtie2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nature Methods 9, 357–359 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3311">35</a></sup>, calling SNPs using FreeBayes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Garrison, E. &amp; Marth, G. Haplotype-based variant detection from short-read sequencing. Preprint at 
                    http://arxiv.org/abs/1207.3907
                    
                   (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3315">36</a></sup> and annotating the SNPs using SnpEff<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Cingolani, P. et al. A program for annotating and predicting the effects of single nucleotide polymorphisms, SnpEff. Fly 6, 80–92 (2014)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3319">37</a></sup>.</p><p>The genome structural annotation was performed using Maker-P<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Campbell, M. S. et al. MAKER-P: a tool kit for the rapid creation, management, and quality control of plant genome annotations. Plant Physiol. 164, 513–524 (2014)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3326">38</a></sup>: (1) SNAP and Augustus as <i>ab initio</i> gene predictors; (2) Exonerate as experimental based predictor with 454 and Illumina RNASeq reads and protein sequences from different protein datasets. RNAseq Illumina data was mapped using Tophat2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105–1111 (2009)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3333">39</a></sup>. tRNAs were annotated using tRNAscan (<a href="http://lowelab.ucsc.edu/tRNAscan-SE/">http://lowelab.ucsc.edu/tRNAscan-SE/</a>).</p><p>The gene functional annotation was performed by sequence homology search with different protein datasets using BlastP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 3389–3402 (1997)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3347">40</a></sup> and protein domains search using InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Mulder, N. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterPro and InterProScan Vol. 396, 59–70 (Humana, 2007)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3351">41</a></sup>. Functional annotations were integrated using AHRD (<a href="https://github.com/groupschoof/AHRD">https://github.com/groupschoof/AHRD</a>). See <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#MOESM9">Supplementary Note 1</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Repetitive elements analysis</h3><p>Repeat annotation was performed using RepeatModeler (v1.0.8; <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html</a>), RepeatMasker (v4.0.5; <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>) with the repeat database Repbase (release 20140131; <a href="http://www.girinst.org/repbase/">http://www.girinst.org/repbase/</a>) and Geneious (v7.1.4; <a href="http://www.geneious.com">http://www.geneious.com</a>). Identification of PVCV-like and EPRV elements was performed using BlastN and TBlastN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 3389–3402 (1997)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3401">40</a></sup>. The identified sequences were aligned with ClustalW (MEGA5 package; <a href="http://www.megasoftware.net/">http://www.megasoftware.net/</a>) and then manually curated. RepeatExplorer (<a href="http://www.repeatexplorer.org/">http://www.repeatexplorer.org/</a>) and other methods were used to extend the analysis to unassembled repeats. Fluorescent <i>in situ</i> hybridization was performed in root tips from young <i>P. axillaris</i> and <i>P. inflata</i> plants for 5S rDNA and three PVCV viral probes following the procedure described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201674#MOESM9">Supplementary Note 2</a>.</p><p>The detection of <i>dTph1</i> loci in <i>P. hybrida</i> W138 was performed through a BLAST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 3389–3402 (1997)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3441">40</a></sup> search of the <i>P. axillaris</i> and <i>P. inflata dTph1</i> elements including the 500 bp of flanking sequence against the TFS W138 collection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Van den Broeck, D. et al. Transposon display identifies individual transposable elements in high copy number lines. Plant J. 13, 121–129 (1998)." href="/articles/nplants201674#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d19403e3452">43</a></sup>.
